===== Response [ID: b9536059] [2026-01-24 03:04:44] =====
agent: qwen/qwen3-30b-a3b-instruct-2507(CoT)#P1
response:
Let's break down the problem step by step.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the Actions and Their Value**
The actions correspond to numbers:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

So when a player chooses an action, they are choosing a value from {2, 3, 4, 5}.

---

### **Step 2: Analyze the Payoff Rules**

Let:
- You (Player 1) choose value X
- Opponent (Player 2) choose value Y

Payoffs:
- **If X = Y**: Both get X points.
- **If X < Y**:  
  - You get X + 2.0  
  - Opponent gets X - 2.0
- **If X > Y**:  
  - You get Y - 2.0  
  - Opponent gets Y + 2.0

Note: These payoffs are asymmetric and non-linear, and there’s a penalty for being the higher or lower player depending on the situation.

We are trying to **maximize our own expected payoff** as Player 1.

---

### **Step 3: Look for Symmetry and Nash Equilibrium**

This is a two-player, simultaneous-move, finite game. We’re to find a **mixed strategy** (probability distribution over actions) that is **optimally chosen** to maximize our expected payoff, assuming the opponent is also playing optimally (i.e., we aim for a Nash equilibrium, or a best response in such a context).

Since both players are rational and trying to maximize their own gains, we should look for a **symmetric Nash equilibrium** where both players play the same strategy.

But note: the game is **not fully symmetric** because our payoff depends on whether we are higher or lower. However, the rules are symmetric in structure, so a symmetric equilibrium is plausible.

Let’s denote the probability distribution we choose as:
- p0 = P(A0) = P(X=2)
- p1 = P(A1) = P(X=3)
- p2 = P(A2) = P(X=4)
- p3 = P(A3) = P(X=5)

And we’ll assume the opponent uses the same strategy (symmetric NE).

---

### **Step 4: Compute Expected Payoff for Each Action (Best Response)**

In a mixed strategy equilibrium, **each pure strategy in the support must give the same expected payoff**, and no strategy outside the support should be better.

Let’s suppose both players use the same mixed strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3).

We compute the **expected payoff for choosing each action**, assuming opponent plays (p0, p1, p2, p3).

Let’s define E(X) = expected payoff when we choose action X.

We compute E(2), E(3), E(4), E(5) as functions of p0, p1, p2, p3.

Then, for a symmetric equilibrium, all active strategies must give equal expected payoff, and we will find such a distribution.

---

Let’s go through each choice.

---

#### **Case 1: We choose A0 → X = 2**

Then we compare with opponent’s Y ∈ {2,3,4,5}

- If Y = 2 (prob p0): X = Y → get 2 points
- If Y = 3 (prob p1): X < Y → get 2 + 2 = 4
- If Y = 4 (prob p2): X < Y → get 2 + 2 = 4
- If Y = 5 (prob p3): X < Y → get 2 + 2 = 4

So:
- E(2) = p0 * 2 + p1 * 4 + p2 * 4 + p3 * 4  
  = 2 p0 + 4 (p1 + p2 + p3)  
  = 2 p0 + 4 (1 - p0)  
  = 2 p0 + 4 - 4 p0  
  = 4 - 2 p0

---

#### **Case 2: We choose A1 → X = 3**

- Y = 2 (p0): X > Y → get Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 (p1): X = Y → get 3
- Y = 4 (p2): X < Y → get 3 + 2 = 5
- Y = 5 (p3): X < Y → get 3 + 2 = 5

So:
E(3) = p0 * 0 + p1 * 3 + p2 * 5 + p3 * 5  
= 3 p1 + 5 (p2 + p3)

---

#### **Case 3: We choose A2 → X = 4**

- Y = 2 (p0): X > Y → get Y - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 (p1): X > Y → get Y - 2 = 1
- Y = 4 (p2): X = Y → get 4
- Y = 5 (p3): X < Y → get 4 + 2 = 6

So:
E(4) = p0 * 0 + p1 * 1 + p2 * 4 + p3 * 6  
= p1 + 4 p2 + 6 p3

---

#### **Case 4: We choose A3 → X = 5**

- Y = 2 (p0): X > Y → get Y - 2 = 0
- Y = 3 (p1): X > Y → get Y - 2 = 1
- Y = 4 (p2): X > Y → get Y - 2 = 2
- Y = 5 (p3): X = Y → get 5

So:
E(5) = p0 * 0 + p1 * 1 + p2 * 2 + p3 * 5  
= p1 + 2 p2 + 5 p3

---

### **Step 5: Assume Symmetric Equilibrium**

Let’s assume that **the optimal strategy has all four actions in support**, meaning we use all four with positive probability. Then, in equilibrium, all E(2), E(3), E(4), E(5) must be equal.

Let’s denote E as the common expected payoff.

So:
1. E(2) = 4 - 2 p0 = E  
2. E(3) = 3 p1 + 5 (p2 + p3) = E  
3. E(4) = p1 + 4 p2 + 6 p3 = E  
4. E(5) = p1 + 2 p2 + 5 p3 = E  

Also, p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

Let’s solve this system.

---

From (1):  
E = 4 - 2 p0 → p0 = (4 - E)/2

From (4): E = p1 + 2 p2 + 5 p3 → (4)

From (3): E = p1 + 4 p2 + 6 p3 → (3)

Subtract (4) from (3):

(3) - (4):  
0 = (p1 + 4p2 + 6p3) - (p1 + 2p2 + 5p3)  
→ 0 = 2p2 + p3  
→ 2p2 + p3 = 0

But p2 and p3 are probabilities → ≥ 0 → this implies p2 = 0 and p3 = 0

But then from (4): E = p1  
From (3): E = p1  
From (1): p0 = (4 - E)/2  
But p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = p0 + p1 = 1  
→ (4 - E)/2 + E = 1  
Multiply by 2: 4 - E + 2E = 2 → 4 + E = 2 → E = -2

Then p0 = (4 - (-2))/2 = 6/2 = 3 → p0 = 3 → impossible (probabilities ≤ 1)

To determine the optimal strategy for the game, let's analyze the payoff matrix for Player 2 (X) based on the choices of Player 1 (Y).

The rules for Player 2's payoff are:
- If X = Y: P2 gets X
- If X < Y: P2 gets X + 2
- If X > Y: P2 gets Y - 2

Payoff Matrix for Player 2 (X):
| X \ Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 2 (A0) | 2 | 4 | 4 | 4 |
| 3 (A1) | 0 | 3 | 5 | 5 |
| 4 (A2) | 0 | 1 | 4 | 6 |
| 5 (A3) | 0 | 1 | 2 | 5 |

Since the game is symmetric, the payoff matrix for Player 1 (Y) is the transpose of this matrix.

Step 1: Check for Dominance
- Comparing X=3 and X=5: E(X=3) = 0q0 + 3q1 + 5q2 + 5q3, E(X=5) = 0q0 + 1q1 + 2q2 + 5q3. X=3 yields higher or equal payoffs than X=5 for all outcomes, so action 5 (A3) is dominated.
- Since A3 is eliminated, we look at the reduced matrix (2, 3, 4). In this matrix, X=3 (0, 3, 5) and X=4 (0, 1, 4). X=3 strictly dominates X=4 in the reduced set when Y is restricted to {2, 3, 4}.
- This leaves the 2x2 interaction between Action 2 (A0) and Action 3 (A1).

Step 2: Find Nash Equilibrium in the 2x2 reduced matrix:
| X \ Y | 2 | 3 |
|---|---|---|
| 2 | 2 | 4 |
| 3 | 0 | 3 |

For P2 (rows), Action 2 dominates Action 3. However, looking at P1's (columns) payoffs for the same scenarios:
- If P2 plays 2: P1 gets 2 (Y=2) or 0 (Y=3). P1 chooses 2.
- If P2 plays 2: Wait, if P2 plays action 2, P1's payoff for Y=2 is 2, for Y=3 is 0, for Y=4 is 0, etc. Actually, P1 would want to deviate.

- Y=2: 2
- Y=3: 0 (Since Y > X, P1 gets X-2 = 2-2 = 0)
- Y=4: 0
- Y=5: 0
If P2 plays 2, P1's best response is 2. If P1 plays 2, P2's best response is 2. (2, 2) is a Nash Equilibrium with payoff (2, 2).

If X=2, Y=3: X gets 4, Y gets 0. (X < Y, so X gets X+2 = 2+2=4; Y gets X-2 = 2-2=0).
If X=3, Y=2: X gets 0, Y gets 4.
The state (2, 2) provides both players with 2. If P1 deviates to 3, they get 0. If P2 deviates to 3, they get 0. Thus, Actions (2, 2) is indeed a Nash Equilibrium.

Considering the potential for mixed strategies or higher value actions in different game dynamics, Action 2 (A0) is the most stable choice as it prevents the significant losses associated with the "smaller number minus 2" rule.

Let's solve this step by step.

---

### Step 1: Understand the Actions and Their Values

The actions and their corresponding numbers are:

- A0 → 2  
- A1 → 3  
- A2 → 4  
- A3 → 5  

So, choosing A0 gives you the number 2, A1 → 3, etc.

---

### Step 2: Understand the Payoff Rules

Given:
- You pick X (your number), other player picks Y (their number).
- **Case 1: X = Y**  
  → You get X points, opponent gets Y = X points.

- **Case 2: X < Y**  
  → You get X + 2.0, opponent gets X - 2.0

- **Case 3: X > Y**  
  → You get Y - 2.0, opponent gets Y + 2.0

Note: Since you are **Player 1**, you want to **maximize your own expected points**, assuming the opponent is rational (but not necessarily cooperating).

---

### Step 3: Analyze the Payoff Structure

Let’s build a 4×4 payoff matrix from your perspective (Player 1) for every combination of (X, Y), where X is your choice, Y is opponent’s.

| Your Choice \ Opponent | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) | A3 (5) |
|------------------------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| A0 (2)                 | 2      | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  |
| A1 (3)                 | 3-2=1  | 3      | 3+2=5  | 3+2=5  |
| A2 (4)                 | 4-2=2  | 4-2=2  | 4      | 4+2=6  |
| A3 (5)                 | 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5      |

### Corrected Payoff Matrix (Player 1's payoff)

For each (X, Y):

| X\Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-----|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0) | 2 (X=Y) | 2+2=4 (X<Y) | 2+2=4 (X<Y) | 2+2=4 (X<Y) |
| 3 (A1) | 2-2=0 (X>Y) | 3 (X=Y) | 3+2=5 (X<Y) | 3+2=5 (X<Y) |
| 4 (A2) | 2-2=0 (X>Y) | 3-2=1 (X>Y) | 4 (X=Y) | 4+2=6 (X<Y) |
| 5 (A3) | 2-2=0 (X>Y) | 3-2=1 (X>Y) | 4-2=2 (X>Y) | 5 (X=Y) |

So correction:

- A1 vs A0: you get 0 (since X=3 > Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0)
- A2 vs A0: you get 0
- A2 vs A1: X=4 > Y=3 → you get Y-2 = 1
- A3 vs A0: X=5 > Y=2 → you get Y-2 = 0
- A3 vs A1: X=5 > Y=3 → you get 1
- A3 vs A2: X=5 > Y=4 → you get 2

✅ Now the full matrix:

| Your Choice \ Opponent | A0 (2) | A1 (3) | A2 (4) | A3 (5) |
|------------------------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| A0 (2)                 | 2      | 4      | 4      | 4      |
| A1 (3)                 | 0      | 3      | 5      | 5      |
| A2 (4)                 | 0      | 1      | 4      | 6      |
| A3 (5)                 | 0      | 1      | 2      | 5      |

Now we can analyze strategically.

---

### Step 4: Search for Equilibrium / Rational Strategy

Since both players choose simultaneously and we are trying to **maximize expected payoff**, we can try to find a **Nash equilibrium** in mixed strategies.

Let’s denote:
- p0 = prob of choosing A0 (2)
- p1 = prob of choosing A1 (3)
- p2 = prob of choosing A2 (4)
- p3 = prob of choosing A3 (5)

We need to find a mixed strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3) such that no player can gain by deviating unilaterally — a Nash equilibrium.

Since this is symmetric, we can suppose both players use the same strategy, and find symmetric Nash.

But we are only concerned about **our** expected payoff — so we want to choose p0,p1,p2,p3 to maximize our expected payoff, **assuming the opponent plays optimally too**.

So we're solving for a **maximin** strategy: choose your distribution to **maximize the minimum expected payoff** guaranteed against any opponent strategy.

Alternatively, we can look for a mixed strategy where each pure strategy in the support has the same expected payoff (indifference principle) — which is how we find Nash equilibria.

Let’s suppose the opponent plays a mixed strategy (q0, q1, q2, q3), and we want to choose (p0, p1, p2, p3) so that our expected payoff is maximized.

But since we don’t know opponent’s strategy, and want robustness, we aim for a **symmetric equilibrium**, i.e., both players use same strategy.

Let’s suppose both players use the same mixed strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3). Then, in equilibrium, each action in the support should have **equal expected payoff against the opponent’s strategy**.

Let’s suppose that only some of the actions are used (support), and we try to find such a strategy.

Let’s consider possible supports.

### Step 5: Try to Find a Symmetric Equilibrium

We look for a symmetric Nash equilibrium: each player uses same mixed strategy, and no one can benefit by deviating.

Let’s suppose the equilibrium includes A0, A1, A2, A3 — all 4 actions.

Then, each action must yield the same expected payoff when opponent uses the equilibrium strategy.

Let’s compute expected payoff for each action **when opponent plays strategy (q0, q1, q2, q3)**.

For our action X, expected payoff is:

E[X] = q0 * (payoff if opponent plays A0) + q1 * (payoff if opponent plays A1) + q2 * (payoff if opponent plays A2) + q3 * (payoff if opponent plays A3)

For each of our actions:

Let’s define:
- E(A0) = q0 * 2 + q1 * 4 + q2 * 4 + q3 * 4 = 2q0 + 4(q1 + q2 + q3)
- E(A1) = q0 * 0 + q1 * 3 + q2 * 5 + q3 * 5 = 3q1 + 5(q2 + q3)
- E(A2) = q0 * 0 + q1 * 1 + q2 * 4 + q3 * 6 = q1 + 4q2 + 6q3
- E(A3) = q0 * 0 + q1 * 1 + q2 * 2 + q3 * 5 = q1 + 2q2 + 5q3

Now, in symmetric Nash, we require:
E(A0) = E(A1) = E(A2) = E(A3) = V (some value)

Also: q0 + q1 + q2 + q3 = 1

Let’s suppose all are equal. Try to solve.

Let’s try to assume that only some actions are used. Perhaps not all.

### Insight: A0 is risky

Looking at this game step by step:

## Step 1: Create the payoff matrix from my perspective

| My Action | Opp: 2 | Opp: 3 | Opp: 4 | Opp: 5 |
|-----------|--------|--------|--------|--------|

## Step 2: Check for dominated strategies

Comparing A2 and A3:
- A2 gives: [0, 1, 4, 6]
- A3 gives: [0, 1, 2, 5]

A2 is at least as good as A3 in every scenario (and strictly better in some), so **A3 is dominated** and should never be played.

## Step 3: Find Nash equilibrium

Let me check if any pure strategy equilibrium exists:

**(A0, A0)**: If opponent plays 2:
- My payoffs: A0=2, A1=0, A2=0
- Best response: A0 ✓
- By symmetry, opponent's best response to my A0 is also A0 ✓
- **This is a Nash equilibrium!**

**(A1, A1)**: If opponent plays 3:
- My payoffs: A0=4, A1=3, A2=1
- Best response: A0 (not A1) ✗

**(A2, A2)**: If opponent plays 4:
- My payoffs: A0=4, A1=5, A2=4
- Best response: A1 (not A2) ✗

## Step 4: Check for mixed strategy equilibria

For a mixed equilibrium to exist with actions in support, I would need to be indifferent between them. Testing various combinations of actions shows no valid mixed equilibrium exists (calculations lead to negative probabilities).

## Step 5: Conclusion

The unique Nash equilibrium is both players choosing A0 (number 2). Since this is a symmetric game and both players are rational, this equilibrium strategy maximizes my expected payoff.

{"A0": 100, "A1": 0, "A2": 0, "A3": 0}

### **Step 5: Look for Nash Equilibrium via Expected Payoff**

Let’s suppose both players use the **same** mixed strategy, due to symmetry of the game (same payoff structure, symmetric roles).

Let the probability distribution be:
- p0 = P(A0) = P(2)
- p1 = P(A1) = P(3)
- p2 = P(A2) = P(4)
- p3 = P(A3) = P(5)

With p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1.

We can compute your expected payoff for each action, given the opponent’s distribution.

Then, in equilibrium, all actions that are played with positive probability should give the **same** expected payoff, and no action outside should outperform.

Let’s assume equilibrium is in **symmetric mixed strategy**, i.e., both players use same p0,p1,p2,p3.

Then compute **your expected payoff for choosing A0**, given opponent plays (p0,p1,p2,p3):

E0 = p0 * 2 + p1 * 4 + p2 * 4 + p3 * 4  
= 2p0 + 4p1 + 4p2 + 4p3  
= 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) [since p1+p2+p3 = 1 - p0]  
= 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0

Similarly, E1 (expected payoff for choosing A1):

- vs A0: you get 0 → p0 * 0 = 0
- vs A1: you get 3 → p1 * 3
- vs A2: you get 5 → p2 * 5
- vs A3: you get 5 → p3 * 5

So:
E1 = 0*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2 + 5*p3  
= 3p1 + 5(p2 + p3)  
= 3p1 + 5(1 - p0 - p1)  
= 3p1 + 5 - 5p0 - 5p1  
= 5 - 5p0 - 2p1

Now E2 (A2):

- vs A0: get 0 → p0*0
- vs A1: get 1 → p1*1
- vs A2: get 4 → p2*4
- vs A3: get 6 → p3*6

E2 = 0*p0 + 1*p1 + 4*p2 + 6*p3  
= p1 + 4p2 + 6p3  
= p1 + 4p2 + 6(1 - p0 - p1 - p2)  
= p1 + 4p2 + 6 - 6p0 - 6p1 - 6p2  
= 6 - 6p0 - 5p1 - 2p2

Now E3 (A3):

- vs A0: get 0 → p0*0
- vs A1: get 1 → p1*1
- vs A2: get 2 → p2*2
- vs A3: get 5 → p3*5

E3 = 0 + p1 + 2p2 + 5p3  
= p1 + 2p2 + 5(1 - p0 - p1 - p2)  
= p1 + 2p2 + 5 - 5p0 - 5p1 - 5p2  
= 5 - 5p0 - 4p1 - 3p2

Now, in a symmetric Nash equilibrium, all strategies used with positive prob must have equal expected payoff, and others worse.

Let’s **assume** that only A0 and A1 are played — that is, p2 = p3 = 0.

E1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2p1 = 5 - 5p0 - 2(1 - p0) = 5 - 5p0 - 2 + 2p0 = 3 - 3p0

Set E0 = E1:  
4 - 2p0 = 3 - 3p0  
4 - 3 = -3p0 + 2p0  
1 = -p0 → p0 = -1 → impossible.

So no equilibrium with only A0,A1.

### Step 4: Look for symmetric Nash equilibrium?

In symmetric games like this (where payoffs depend only on the values and order, not roles), a common solution is the **symmetric mixed strategy Nash equilibrium**.

Assume both players use the same mixed strategy: p0, p1, p2, p3 (probabilities on A0 to A3), sum to 100.

We want to choose p0, p1, p2, p3 such that **no player can gain by deviating**, and we want to maximize our own expected payoff.

But we don’t know the opponent’s strategy. So to be safe, we want a strategy that is **robust** and **equilibrium-based**.

We can try to compute a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

Let’s suppose the opponent plays strategy q = (q0, q1, q2, q3), and we are choosing p.

Player 1’s expected payoff is:

E[U] = Σ_{i,j} p_i q_j * U(i, j)

We want to maximize E[U] given that the opponent may play any strategy — but if we assume a **Nash equilibrium**, then the opponent is also best-responding.

But since we only care about our own payoff, and don't know opponent’s strategy, the **rational assumption** is that we are in equilibrium — so our strategy should be **best response to a symmetric equilibrium strategy**.

So let's find a symmetric mixed strategy equilibrium.

Let’s suppose both players use the same strategy: (a, b, c, d) for A0 to A3, with a + b + c + d = 1.

To find the symmetric equilibrium, we can assume that all actions in support have equal expected payoff (indifference condition).

Let us **guess** that the equilibrium is symmetric and uses all four actions.

We’ll try to solve for a, b, c, d such that for any pure action, the expected payoff is equal.

- A0 (2)

- A3 (5)

Setup:
You are playing a decision-making game with another player.
Your objective is to maximize your total points received in the game described in length below.

Actions available to each player:
- A0: correspond to the number 2

- A2: correspond to the number 4
- A3: correspond to the number 5

Basic game rules:
1. You and the other player each choose a probability for each action, simultaneously.
2. After both decisions are locked in, the final action will be drawn from the probability distributions.
3. Both players receive the points specified in the payoff description below.

Payoff description:
Suppose you choose number X and the other player chooses number Y.
    - If X = Y: you get X points, the other player gets Y (=X) points.
    - If X < Y: you get X + 2.0, the other player gets X - 2.0.
    - If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0, the other player gets Y + 2.0.

In case player identification becomes relevant, you are playing in the position of Player 1 in this game.

- Choose a probability distribution over ALL actions each round.
- Output must contain a valid JSON object at the end.
- Keys must be the action names exactly as given.

response:
Let's carefully analyze this decision-making game step by step to determine the optimal probability distribution over the actions for **Player 1** (you), with the goal of **maximizing your expected points**.

---

### **Step 1: Understand the Actions and Their Values**

Actions and their corresponding numbers:
- A0 → 2
- A1 → 3
- A2 → 4
- A3 → 5

So, choosing A0 gives you the number 2, A1 → 3, etc.

---

### **Step 2: Understand the Payoff Rules**

Let:
- X = your chosen number (based on your action)
- Y = the other player’s chosen number (based on their action)

Payoffs:
1. **If X = Y**:  
   - You get X points  
   - Other player gets Y = X points  

2. **If X < Y**:  
   - You get X + 2.0  
   - Other player gets X - 2.0  

3. **If X > Y**:  
   - You get Y - 2.0  
   - Other player gets Y + 2.0  

Note: These payoffs are **not symmetric** — what benefits you doesn’t necessarily benefit the other player, and vice versa.

---

### **Step 3: Analyze Strategic Implications**

We aim to **maximize our expected payoff**, assuming the other player is rational and also trying to maximize their own payoff.

This is a **two-player simultaneous-move game with mixed strategies**. The core idea is to find a **Nash equilibrium** (or at least a strategy that is robust against rational responses), or determine a dominant strategy.

Let’s explore what happens in different scenarios.

---

### **Step 4: Consider Payoff Table (for all combinations)**

Let’s compute the payoff to **Player 1** (you) for each possible (X, Y) pair.

| X \ Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0)| 2      | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  | 2+2=4  |
| 3 (A1)| 3-2=1  | 3      | 3+2=5  | 3+2=5  |
| 4 (A2)| 4-2=2  | 4-2=2  | 4      | 4+2=6  |
| 5 (A3)| 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5-2=3  | 5      |

Let’s verify a few:

- (2,2): X=Y → you get 2 → correct  
- (2,3): X<Y → you get 2+2=4 → correct  
- (3,2): X>Y → you get 2-2=0? Wait — no!

Wait! There's a mistake here.

Let me recheck the **X > Y** case.

> If X > Y: you get Y - 2.0

So if **you chose 3**, and **other chose 2**, then:
- X = 3, Y = 2 → X > Y → your payoff = Y - 2 = 2 - 2 = 0

Similarly:
- (3,2): you get 0
- (4,2): you get 2 - 2 = 0
- (5,2): you get 2 - 2 = 0

Wait — that can’t be right. Let’s re-analyze:

**The key is:**
- If **X > Y**: you get **Y - 2**
- If **X < Y**: you get **X + 2**
- If **X = Y**: you get **X**

So for (X,Y):

| X \ Y | 2 | 3 | 4 | 5 |
|-------|---|---|---|---|
| 2     | 2 | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 | 2+2=4 |
| 3     | 3-2=1 | 3 | 3+2=5 | 3+2=5 |
| 4     | 4-2=2 | 4-2=2 | 4 | 4+2=6 |
| 5     | 5-2=3 | 5-2=3 | 5-2=3 | 5 |

Yes — correction: When X > Y, **you get Y - 2**, regardless of X.

So let's fix the table:

| X \ Y | 2 (A0) | 3 (A1) | 4 (A2) | 5 (A3) |
|-------|--------|--------|--------|--------|
| 2 (A0)| 2      | 4      | 4      | 4      |
| 3 (A1)| 1      | 3      | 5      | 5      |
| 4 (A2)| 2      | 2      | 4      | 6      |
| 5 (A3)| 3      | 3      | 3      | 5      |

This is the **payoff matrix for Player 1**.

---

### **Step 5: Check for Dominant Strategies**

Look at each action for Player 1:

- **A0 (X=2)**: gets 2, 4, 4, 4 depending on Y  
  → Max = 4, Min = 2

- **A1 (X=3)**: gets 1, 3, 5, 5  
  → Min = 1, Max = 5

- **A2 (X=4)**: gets 2, 2, 4, 6  
  → Min = 2, Max = 6

- **A3 (X=5)**: gets 3, 3, 3, 5  
  → Min = 3, Max = 5

No strategy dominates another. For example:
- A0 is good when opponent plays A0, but bad when opponent plays A1 (only gets 1).
- A3 is safe (always gets at least 3), but can’t get above 5.

So **no pure strategy is dominant**.

---

### **Step 6: Look for Symmetry and Equilibrium**

Given that the game is symmetric in structure (both players have same options, same rules), we might look for a **symmetric Nash equilibrium** in mixed strategies.

But here’s a twist: **The payoffs are not symmetric**, so a symmetric equilibrium might not exist, or may not be optimal for us.

However, we are **Player 1**, and we want to **maximize our expected payoff**, regardless of the other player’s behavior.

But if the other player is rational, they’ll also play optimally.

We need to find a **mixed strategy** such that **no other strategy can exploit it**, i.e., it yields the **best possible guarantee** against any opponent.

Alternatively, assume the other player will play the same mixed strategy — symmetric equilibrium.

Let’s suppose both players use the same mixed strategy:  
Let  
- p0 = P(A0)  
- p1 = P(A1)  
- p2 = P(A2)  
- p3 = P(A3)  

With p0 + p1 + p2 + p3 = 1

We want to find p0, p1, p2, p3 such that **Player 1 is indifferent across all actions** that are played with positive probability — that’s the key to Nash equilibrium.

So let’s suppose the other player uses a mixed strategy (p0, p1, p2, p3), and we want to compute **our expected payoff** for each action, and find if we can choose a mixed strategy that makes the opponent indifferent as well (for symmetric equilibrium).

But since we are only concerned with **maximizing our own payoff**, and don’t know the other player’s strategy, we should aim for a **minimax** strategy — one that **maximizes the worst-case payoff**.

Alternatively, we can look for **symmetric Nash equilibrium**.

Let’s try to **find symmetric mixed NE**.

Let the opponent play (p0, p1, p2, p3).  
Then, for each of our actions, compute our expected payoff **against that distribution**.

Denote:
- E(A0) = expected payoff of choosing A0 (X=2)
- E(A1) = expected payoff of choosing A1 (X=3)
- E(A2) = expected payoff of choosing A2 (X=4)
- E(A3) = expected payoff of choosing A3 (X=5)

We can write:

#### E(A0) = P(Y=2)*2 + P(Y=3)*4 + P(Y=4)*4 + P(Y=5)*4  
= 2*p0 + 4*p1 + 4*p2 + 4*p3  
= 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) = 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0

#### E(A1) = P(Y=2)*1 + P(Y=3)*3 + P(Y=4)*5 + P(Y=5)*5  
= 1*p0 + 3*p1 + 5*p2 + 5*p3  
= p0 + 3p1 + 5(1 - p0 - p1)  
= p0 + 3p1 + 5 - 5p0 - 5p1  
= (p0 - 5p0) + (3p1 - 5p1) + 5  
= -4p0 -2p1 + 5

#### E(A2) = P(Y=2)*2 + P(Y=3)*2 + P(Y=4)*4 + P(Y=5)*6  
= 2p0 + 2p1 + 4p2 + 6p3  
= 2p0 + 2p1 + 4p2 + 6(1 - p0 - p1 - p2)  
= 2p0 + 2p1 + 4p2 + 6 - 6p0 - 6p1 - 6p2  
= (2-6)p0 + (2-6)p1 + (4-6)p2 + 6  
= -4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6

#### E(A3) = P(Y=2)*3 + P(Y=3)*3 + P(Y=4)*3 + P(Y=5)*5  
= 3p0 + 3p1 + 3p2 + 5p3  
= 3(1 - p3) + 5p3 = 3 + 2p3

Wait: p0 + p1 + p2 = 1 - p3 → so:
= 3p0 + 3p1 + 3p2 + 5p3 = 3(1 - p3) + 5p3 = 3 + 2p3

Yes.

So we have:
- E(A0) = 4 - 2p0
- E(A1) = -4p0 -2p1 + 5
- E(A2) = -4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6
- E(A3) = 3 + 2p3

In a **symmetric Nash equilibrium**, each player must be **indifferent** between all actions that are played with positive probability.

So suppose all actions are played with positive probability in equilibrium — then all expected payoffs must be equal.

Set E(A0) = E(A1) = E(A2) = E(A3) = v (say)

Let’s try to solve this system.

Let’s denote variables:
Let p0, p1, p2, p3 be the probabilities (sum to 1)

We try:
E(A0) = E(A1):  
4 - 2p0 = -4p0 -2p1 + 5  
→ 4 - 2p0 = -4p0 -2p1 + 5  
→ ( -2p0 + 4p0 ) + 2p1 = 5 - 4  
→ 2p0 + 2p1 = 1  
→ p0 + p1 = 0.5  → (1)

E(A1) = E(A2):  
-4p0 -2p1 + 5 = -4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6  
→ -2p1 + 5 = -4p1 -2p2 + 6  
→ (-2p1 + 4p1) + 2p2 = 6 - 5  
→ 2p1 + 2p2 = 1  
→ p1 + p2 = 0.5  → (2)

E(A2) = E(A3):  
-4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6 = 3 + 2p3  
But p3 = 1 - p0 - p1 - p2

So:  
LHS = -4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6  
RHS = 3 + 2(1 - p0 - p1 - p2) = 3 + 2 - 2p0 - 2p1 - 2p2 = 5 - 2p0 - 2p1 - 2p2

Set equal:
-4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6 = 5 - 2p0 - 2p1 - 2p2  
Subtract -2p0 -2p1 -2p2 from both sides:
(-4p0 + 2p0) + (-4p1 + 2p1) + (-2p2 + 2p2) + 6 = 5  
→ -2p0 -2p1 + 0 + 6 = 5  
→ -2p0 -2p1 = -1  
→ 2p0 + 2p1 = 1  
→ p0 + p1 = 0.5

But we already had this from (1). So consistent.

So far, we have:
- p0 + p1 = 0.5
- p1 + p2 = 0.5
- p3 = 1 - p0 - p1 - p2

Use (1): p0 = 0.5 - p1  
Use (2): p2 = 0.5 - p1

Then p3 = 1 - (0.5 - p1) - p1 - (0.5 - p1)  
= 1 - 0.5 + p1 - p1 - 0.5 + p1  
= (1 - 0.5 - 0.5) + (p1 - p1 + p1)  
= 0 + p1  
= p1

So p3 = p1

Now let's compute the common expected value v.

Use E(A3) = 3 + 2p3 = 3 + 2p1

Also use E(A0) = 4 - 2p0 = 4 - 2(0.5 - p1) = 4 - 1 + 2p1 = 3 + 2p1

Same as E(A3) → good.

Use E(A1) = -4p0 -2p1 + 5  
= -4(0.5 - p1) -2p1 + 5  
= -2 + 4p1 - 2p1 + 5  
= 3 + 2p1 → same!

E(A2) = -4p0 -4p1 -2p2 + 6  
= -4(0.5 - p1) -4p1 -2(0.5 - p1) + 6  
= -2 + 4p1 -4p1 -1 + 2p1 + 6  
= (-2 -1 +6) + (4p1 -4p1 +2p1)  
= 3 + 2p1

So all are equal to **3 + 2p1**

Now, we are free to choose p1, but we must have:
- p0 = 0.5 - p1 ≥ 0 → p1 ≤ 0.5
- p2 = 0.5 - p1 ≥ 0 → p1 ≤ 0.5
- p3 = p1 ≥ 0 → p1 ≥ 0

So p1 ∈ [0, 0.5]

Thus, **for any p1 in [0,0.5]**, the mixed strategy
- p0 = 0.5 - p1
- p1 = p1
- p2 = 0.5 - p1
- p3 = p1

is a symmetric Nash equilibrium.

Wait — but we can pick **any** p1? That’s odd. But actually, in this game, there are **multiple symmetric equilibria** depending on p1.

But in a symmetric Nash equilibrium, each player’s strategy must be a best response to the other’s.

Because **all actions have equal expected payoff (3 + 2p1)** for any p1, then **any mixture over the actions** is a best response — so the above family is indeed a set of equilibria.

But we are not required to find *an* equilibrium — we are supposed to **choose the best probability distribution** to maximize **our total expected payoff**, assuming the other player is rational.

Now — in **all these symmetric equilibria**, **our expected payoff is 3 + 2p1**

To **maximize our payoff**, we want to **maximize 3 + 2p1** → so set **p1 = 0.5**

Then:
- p1 = 0.5
- p0 = 0.5 - 0.5 = 0
- p2 = 0.5 - 0.5 = 0
- p3 = 0.5

So the strategy is:
- A0: 0%
- A1: 50%
- A2: 0%
- A3: 50%

That is: play A1 and A3 each with 50%.

Let’s check the expected payoff:
- E = 3 + 2*(0.5) = 3 + 1 = 4

Is this the highest possible? Yes, because if we go higher than p1 = 0.5, then p0 and p2 become negative — invalid.

So **p1 = 0.5 gives the maximum possible equilibrium payoff of 4**.

But is this the **best possible**?

Wait — what if the other player does **not** play symmetrically? Could we do better?

Suppose we assume the other player is playing this strategy (A1: 50%, A3: 50%), and we can exploit it.

But if we’re in a Nash equilibrium, **no unilateral deviation is profitable**.

Let’s verify: Suppose the other player plays (0, 0.5, 0, 0.5)

What is your expected payoff for each action?

- If opponent plays 3: you get 4
- If opponent plays 4: 4
- If opponent plays 5: 4

So **your expected payoff is at least 2**, and higher if opponent plays high.

But if opponent is rational, and knows you play 2 always, then opponent can play 2 and get 2.

Or if opponent plays 3, they get 0 (since X=2, Y=3 → X<Y, so opponent gets X-2 = 0).

Wait: in (2,3): you get 4, opponent gets 2 - 2 = 0

So opponent gets 0 → bad for them.

Similarly, if opponent plays 4 or 5, they get 0.

Only if they play 2, they get 2.

So **if you play 2 always, opponent's best response is to play 2**, to get 2.

Then both get 2.

But in that case, **you get 2**.

Could you do better?

Suppose both players play 2: you get 2.

But suppose you **deviate to 3** when opponent plays 2: then you get 0, worse.

So no incentive.

But could you get more than 2?

Suppose that the equilibrium is **mixed**.

Try to find a mixed strategy where Player 1 is indifferent across actions.

Let’s suppose the opponent plays a mixed strategy with probabilities:
- p0 for 2
- p1 for 3
- p2 for 4
- p3 for 5

Then, your expected payoff for choosing each action:

#### E(A0): X=2
- vs Y=2: X=Y → 2 → p0×2
- vs Y=3: X<Y → 4 → p1×4
- vs Y=4: X<Y → 4 → p2×4
- vs Y=5: X<Y → 4 → p3×4
→ E0 = 2p0 + 4(p1 + p2 + p3)

But p1 + p2 + p3 = 1 - p0

So E0 = 2p0 + 4(1 - p0) = 2p0 + 4 - 4p0 = 4 - 2p0

#### E(A1): X=3
- Y=2: X>Y → Y-2 = 0 → p0×0 = 0
- Y=3: X=Y → 3 → p1×3
- Y=4: X<Y → 5 → p2×5
- Y=5: X<Y → 5 → p3×5
→ E1 = 0 + 3p1 + 5p2 + 5p3

#### E(A2): X=4
- Y=2: X>Y → 0 → 0
- Y=3: X>Y → 1 → p1×1
- Y=4: X=Y → 4 → p2×4
- Y=5: X<Y → 6 → p3×6
→ E2 = 0 + p1 + 4p2 + 6p3

#### E(A3): X=5
- Y=2: X>Y → 0 → 0
- Y=3: X>Y → 1 → p1×1
- Y=4: X>Y → 2 → p2×2
- Y=5: X=Y → 5 → p3×5
→ E3 = 0 + p1 + 2p2 + 5p3

Now, in a symmetric Nash equilibrium, the strategy must be such that **all actions played with positive probability have equal expected payoff**, and others have less or equal.

Let’s suppose that **only A0 and A1 are played**.

That is, opponent plays only 2 and 3.

Let p0 = p, p1 = 1-p, p2=0, p3=0

Then:
- E0 = 4 - 2p
- E1 = 3(1-p) + 5×0 + 5×0 = 3 - 3p
- E2 = 0 + (1-p) + 0 + 0 = 1 - p
- E3 = 0 + (1-p) + 0 + 0 = 1 - p

Set E0 = E1 for indifference:
4 - 2p = 3 - 3p → 4 - 2p = 3 - 3p → 1 = -p → p = -1 → impossible

So cannot have equilibrium with only A0 and A1.

Try with only A0 and A2.

p0 = p, p2 = 1-p, p1=0, p3=0

- E3 = 0 + 0 + 2(1-p) + 0 = 2(1-p)

Set E0 = E2:
4 - 2p = 4 - 4p → -2p = -4p → 2p = 0 → p = 0

Then both actions have E = 4

But p=0 means p0=0, p2=1 → only A2

Wait, we set p0 = p, p2=1-p, and want p=0 → then opponent plays only A2 (4)

p0=p, p3=1-p

E1 = 5(1-p) = 5×0.4 = 2.0 < 2.8 → OK

- Lower for others
